(cid:34)(cid:86)(cid:85)o(cid:78)(cid:66)(cid:85)(cid:74)o(cid:79)Korea Development Bank (KDB) has been the foundation of the nation’s 
economy and financial industry for the past 66 years.
KDB has evolved successfully in line with the changing demands of its 
stakeholders and Korean markets. KDB now inspires to transform into the 
nation’s leading financial platform which materializes a better society.
As we usher in the new era of Fourth Industrial Revolution, KDB will 
provide a strong impetus for a greater sense of responsible management, 
promotion of sustainable finance, and creating the future for a shared 
growth with innovation and creativity.
KDB will turn transactions into connections, connections into 
opportunities, and opportunities into powerful waves of new growth, 
fulfilling its ultimate mandate of an advanced policy bank at the forefront 
of Korea’s sustainable growth.

HIGHLIGHTSCORPORATE BANKING & RESTRUCTURINGCorporate BankingDebt Capital MarketIn 2020, KDB financed in total amount of KRW 91.1 trillion, 63% (or KRW 57.1 trillion) of 
which was extended to small and medium sized enterprises (SMEs) and medium sized 
enterprises (MEs). In particular, the Bank provided KRW 3.57 trillion in 2020 under the 
“Special Fund for Cheering Up Korea” program to SMEs and MEs hit hard by the Covid-19 
pandemic in order to prevent temporary degradation of their businesses from triggering 
a collapse of the industrial ecosystem and leaving a lasting damage in the national 
competitiveness.PROMPT

GROWTHScale-up BankingKDB NextRoundVENTURE FINACEVenture & Technology BankingThe KDB NextRound is a market-driven platform for venture investment that provides 
start-ups with the opportunity to attract investments, and investors with the opportunity 
to discover new investment targets. Since its inauguration in 2016, 430 IR rounds have 
been held for a total of 1,514 companies. The rounds introduced start-ups identified 
by accelerators, venture capital firms, and government-funded research institutes and 
attracted venture capital from both domestic and overseas investors. Among the start-
ups which participated in the rounds, 358 successfully attracted investments totaling 
KRW 2.2 trillion.430325741749(cid:47)(cid:70)(cid:89)(cid:85)(cid:51)o(cid:86)(cid:79)dAccumulated Number of

BANKDe(cid:81)ositsLIABILTY MANAGEMENT(cid:44)DB(cid:62995)BondsKDB pioneered the ESG bonds market by issuing its first foreign currency denominated 
Green Bond in amount of USD 300 million in 2017 and the country’s first ever domestic 
green and social bonds issuances amounting to KRW 600 billion in 2018, and has shown 
its commitment to the global trend of promoting sustainability in finance.
Moreover, KDB strengthened the transparency of disclosure of the social and environmental  
impacts of its ESG bonds. For its foreign currency denominated ESG Bonds, KDB obtained 
a second party opinion for its 2019 KDB Sustainability Bond Framework, and for its 
domestic ESG bonds has obtained an independent third party opinion on the bond 
prospectus. Such efforts will help enhance investor confidence in KDB’s ESG bonds and 
contribute to a qualitative growth of the ESG bond market.

REVIEWKDB has strengthened policy financing support by providing 
funds in a timely and stable manner to aid the recovery of 
the economy which was hit by Covid-19.
In addition, KDB has taken the lead in adopting global norms 
and standards and spread them across the nation not only to 
promote both quantitative expansion but also the qualitative 
growth of green financing based on the Korean New Deal 
and ESG principles.020

in a way that is ethical, responsible, and sustainable. The 
Bank also accelerated its digital transformation by adopting 
innovative technologies such as big data, AI and robotic 
process automation(RPA). KDB is committed to the global 
trend of promoting sustainability and will become the 
leading advanced policy bank at the forefront of Korea’s

and data searc(cid:73)in(cid:72)(cid:15)IT & Digital Transformation053Completion of an advanced staging area for the digital era- 
Dual Digital Square
KDB has established an advanced IT infrastructure in KDB Digital 
Square, a KDB’s new main data center located in Hanam city. 
Furthermore, the Bank’s existing main data center located in 
Yeouido (the headoffice) has been redesigned to accommodate 
high-performance and large-capacity IT systems so that it can serve 
as a disaster recovery center. A new network cabling structure and 
containment system has been set in place to make cooperation 
between the digital square and the main data center more efficient. 
By designing an uninterrupted power supply system and a seismic 
structure for the existing data center, the Bank expects to maintain 
business continuity even in the event of a disaster. 
Moreover, KDB began relocating its computation equipment 
in October 2018, which were previously scattered all over 
the places. The relocation work took place four times for 
approximately 20 months, and at long last, KDB’s solid Dual IT 
Centers were completed in May 2020.Strengthening digital strategies - KDB Digital Way
In 2021, KDB expects an acceleration in the digital innovation 
trends, such as introduction of a data-based economy and 
growth of remote service industries. AI technology, which is 
staged beyond its experimental phase, is expected to be applied 
to numerous industries, making it account for a larger part of 
people’s daily lives. Against this backdrop, financial institutions 
are rapidly adapting to such changes, and competition among 
them is expected to intensify in the digital finance sector.4) Remote business operation
By improving its remote business channels such as Mobile 
Banking UI/UX and Open Banking, KDB substantially enhanced 
the banking experience for its retail customers. For its corporate 
clients, the Bank plans to establish a remote transaction service 
that enables them to complete their loan execution processes 
without physically visiting the Bank. The Bank already 
completed process analysis and legal reviews required for the 
practice and expects the process to be implemented by 2021. 
Spurring innovation in the work culture for the New Normal 
2.0 era - KDB’s digital work environment
For the first time in the domestic financial sector, KDB has 
laid out the foundation for enhancing workplace productivity 
and promoted a culture of paperless reporting by providing 
the latest laptop computers to all employees and installing 
a 6th generation wireless LAN (WIFI6) within the headquarter 
and all of its branches. The Bank fostered the environment 
that enables the staff to work from anywhere and facilitated a 
culture of creative working. 
Furthermore, KDB secured work continuity by implementing 
a work from home system (WFH) amidst the Covid-19 crisis by 
adopting virtual desktop infrastructure (VDI). In so doing, the 
Bank nourished a safe working environment with employees 
utilizing WFH even during non-emergency periods when 
the Covid-19 was less severe. KDB also established a video 
conference system, further promoting an environment of utilizing 
remote communication. By introducing cloud repository, the 
Bank also strengthened knowledge sharing and collaboration.(cid:17)(cid:22)(cid:21) (cid:44)DB(cid:62995)B(cid:34)(cid:47)(cid:44)(cid:62995)2020(cid:62995)(cid:34)(cid:47)(cid:47)(cid:54)(cid:34)(cid:45)(cid:62995)R(cid:38)(cid:49)(cid:48)R(cid:53)(cid:53)o brace (cid:71)or t(cid:73)e u(cid:81)comin(cid:72) c(cid:73)allen(cid:72)es, (cid:44)DB will concentrate its

(cid:44)DB, as (cid:44)orea(cid:719)s re(cid:81)resentative (cid:71)inancial institution wit(cid:73) its stron(cid:72) ca(cid:81)ital ade(cid:82)uacy, (cid:73)as been evaluated and 
reco(cid:72)ni(cid:91)ed by t(cid:73)e ma(cid:75)or international credit ratin(cid:72) a(cid:72)encies(cid:15) In 2020, (cid:44)DB maintained credit ratin(cid:72)s o(cid:71) (cid:34)a2 (cid:71)rom 
(cid:46)oody(cid:719)s, (cid:34)(cid:34) (cid:71)rom S(cid:7)(cid:49) and (cid:34)(cid:34)(cid:14) (cid:71)rom (cid:39)itc(cid:73), and its BIS ca(cid:81)ital ratio stood at 1(cid:22)(cid:15)9(cid:23)(cid:6)(cid:15)0752021 OutlookThe Covid-19 crisis is expected to continue in 2021, but the global economy is expected to show signs of 
recovery with the help of stimulus packages rolled out by major economies like the US and Europe. However, 
a global turnaround may be hampered by several risk factors including delay in production and distribution of 
the Covid-19 vaccines, limited policy options due to steep rise in government debt and delayed improvement in 
employment conditions.
According to the IMF’s World Economic Outlook (2021), world output is expected to increase by 6.0% year-on-
year in 2021. The growth rate of advanced economies is projected to grow 5.1% from the previous year, with the 
US expecting to show a higher growth rate of 6.4% while Eurozone (4.4%) and Japan (3.3%) are projected to 
show a slower rate. Major emerging markets, such as China (+8.4%) and India (+12.5%), are expected to show 
fast pace recoveries.
The Bank of Korea’s forecast for Korea’s growth outlook is 3.0% while the IMF’s projection is 3.6%. The Korean 
economy is expected to show a modest recovery thanks mostly to increase in investments and exports, but 
consumer spending is expected to be slower in recovery. Such forecasts are subject to scrutiny since the effect 
and longevity of the Covid-19 crisis is hard to predict, and as is the case with most other countries, Korea’s 
recovery will heavily depend on the success of the Covid-19 vaccine and the overall recovery of the global 
economy.
To overcome the challenges of 2021, KDB has set its business objective at “Global KDB, leading innovation and 
the future of Korea” with the goal of encouraging the growth of new industries, driving the shift in industrial 
paradigm for the future of Korea, as well as invigorating the economy through Covid-19 crisis management. In 
preparing for future economic transitions, the Bank will usher in a low carbon economy by actively supporting 
the K-New Deal through launching public sector-led New Deal Funds and expanding green financing. The Bank 
will also support innovative future industries by identifying and nurturing companies with creative potential 
and promoting a dynamic venture ecosystem within the country. 
Looking ahead to the post Covid-19 era, KDB will continue operating its Capital Market Stabilization Program 
and lead the stabilization of Korea’s financial markets. The Bank will launch financial support programs to 
minimize default risk of corporate clients from rapid surge in their debt due to the pandemic and provide 
tailored solutions to distressed companies and facilitate their recovery. 
Similar to last year, KDB expects a volatile and unpredictable financial environment in 2021. As Korea’s leading 
policy bank, KDB will continue to carry out its market stabilization role based on its strong credit ratings, 
globally competitive financial expertise, and thorough risk management.(cid:17)(cid:24)6 (cid:44)DB(cid:62995)B(cid:34)(cid:47)(cid:44)(cid:62995)2020(cid:62995)(cid:34)(cid:47)(cid:47)(cid:54)(cid:34)(cid:45)(cid:62995)R(cid:38)(cid:49)(cid:48)R(cid:53)(cid:42)(cid:79)co(cid:78)(cid:70)(cid:1)(cid:34)(cid:79)(cid:66)(cid:77)(cid:90)(cid:84)(cid:74)(cid:84)Summary Statement of Income(cid:47)et o(cid:81)eratin(cid:72) revenue

flo(cid:90) analysis and option pricing models(cid:17)Financial instruments, (cid:90)hich are not actively traded in the market and those (cid:90)ith less transparent market prices,

and variables(cid:17)(ii) Credit losses allowance

3(cid:17)     (cid:54)ignificant (cid:36)ccounting (cid:51)olicies, (cid:38)ontinued(cid:11)(cid:27)(cid:12) Deri(cid:89)ati(cid:89)e financial instruments including hedge accounting 
Derivative  financial  instruments  are  initially  recognised  at  fair  value  upon  agreement  of  the  contract  and  re(cid:16)
estimated at fair value subsequently(cid:17) (cid:55)he recognition of profit or loss due to changes in fair value of derivative 
instruments is as stated belo(cid:90)(cid:29)(i) Hedge accounting 
Derivative financial instruments are accounted differently depending on (cid:90)hether hedge accounting is applied, and 
therefore, are classified into trading purpose derivatives and hedging purpose derivatives(cid:17)  
(cid:56)pon the transaction of hedging purpose derivatives, t(cid:90)o different types of hedge accounting are applied(cid:30) a fair 
value hedge, and a cash flo(cid:90) hedge(cid:17) A fair value hedge is a hedge of the e(cid:91)posure to changes in fair value of a 
recognised asset or liability or an unrecognised firm commitment, or an identified portion of such an asset, liability 
or firm commitment, that is attributable to a particular risk and could affect profit or loss(cid:17) A cash flo(cid:90) hedge is a 
hedge  of  the  e(cid:91)posure  to  variability  in  cash  flo(cid:90)s  that  (i)  is  attributable  to  a  particular  risk  associated  (cid:90)ith  a 
recognised asset or liability (such as all or some future interest payments on variable rate debt) or a highly probable 
forecast transaction and (ii) could affect profit or loss(cid:17) 
 
At inception of the hedge relationship, the (cid:42)roup formally documents the relationship bet(cid:90)een the hedged item and 
the hedging instrument, including the nature of the risk, the objective and strategy for undertaking the hedge, and

the nature of each transaction(cid:17) (cid:55)he (cid:42)roup enters into hedging derivative transactions mainly for the purpose of 
hedging risk related to changes in fair values of the underlying assets and liabilities and future cash flo(cid:90)s(cid:17)  
 
(cid:55)he (cid:42)roup enters into trading derivative transactions such as futures, for(cid:90)ards, s(cid:90)aps and options for arbitrage 
transactions by speculating on the future value of the underlying asset(cid:17) (cid:55)rading derivative transactions include 
contracts (cid:90)ith the (cid:42)roup(cid:182)s clients and its liquidation position(cid:17)  
 
For the purpose of hedging the e(cid:91)posure to the variability of fair values and future cash flo(cid:90)s of funds in Korean 
(cid:90)on by changes in interest rate, the (cid:42)roup mainly uses interest s(cid:90)aps or currency s(cid:90)aps(cid:17) (cid:55)he main counterparties 
are foreign financial institutions and local banks(cid:17) In addition, to hedge the e(cid:91)posure to the variability of fair values 
of bonds in foreign currencies by changes in interest rate or foreign e(cid:91)change rate, the (cid:42)roup mainly uses interest 
s(cid:90)aps or currency s(cid:90)aps(cid:17)  
 
(cid:55)he (cid:42)roup applies net investment hedge accounting by designating non(cid:16)derivative financial instruments as hedging 
instruments  and  any  gain  or  loss  on  the  hedging  instruments  relating  to  the  effective  portion  of  the  hedge  is 
recognised in other comprehensive income and accumulated in the foreign currency translation reserve(cid:17)

recogni(cid:93)ed on the statement of financial position, but are disclosed as off(cid:16)statement of financial position items in 
the notes to the financial statements(cid:17) (cid:55)he (cid:42)roup provides a provision for such off(cid:16)statement of financial position

(i) (cid:55)he (cid:42)roup(cid:182)s policies for measuring fair value of financial instruments at amorti(cid:93)ed costs are as follo(cid:90)s(cid:29)-  Cash and due from banks(cid:29) Fair value of cash is considered equivalent to the carrying amount(cid:17) In the case of 
due from banks on demand, (cid:90)hich do not have a set maturity and can be reali(cid:93)ed instantly, the carrying 
amount is a close estimate of the fair value and is assumed so(cid:17) In the case of other ordinary due from banks, 
the cash flo(cid:90) discount method is used to estimate the fair value(cid:17)-  Securities measured at amorti(cid:93)ed cost(cid:29) (cid:55)he fair value of securities measured at amorti(cid:93)ed cost is computedby (cid:90)idely(cid:16)accepted appraisal agencies upon request(cid:17)-  (cid:47)oans measured at amorti(cid:93)ed cost(cid:29) (cid:55)he fair value of loans measured at amorti(cid:93)ed cost is the e(cid:91)pected future 
cash flo(cid:90)s, reflecting premature redemption ratio, discounted by the market interest rate, adjusted by a spread 
sheet considering the probability of default(cid:17) (cid:40)(cid:91)ceptions to this method include loans (cid:90)ith credit line facilities, 
loans (cid:90)ith a maturity of three months or less left and impaired loans, (cid:90)hich the (cid:42)roup assumes the carrying 
amount as the fair value(cid:17)-  Deposits(cid:29)  (cid:55)he  fair  value  of  deposits  is  computed  using  the  discounted  cash  flo(cid:90)  method(cid:17)  (cid:43)o(cid:90)ever,  for 
deposits, (cid:90)hose cash flo(cid:90)s cannot be estimated reasonably, the Bank assumes the carrying amount as the fair 
value(cid:17)-  Borro(cid:90)ings(cid:29) (cid:55)he fair value of industrial financial debentures is computed using the discounted cash flo(cid:90) 
method by the (cid:42)roup(cid:182)s Fair (cid:57)alue (cid:40)valuation System(cid:17) (cid:43)o(cid:90)ever, for borro(cid:90)ings including call money (cid:90)hose 
contractual maturity is three months or less, the (cid:42)roup assumes the carrying amount as the fair value(cid:17)-  Debentures(cid:29) (cid:55)he fair value of industrial financial debentures is computed using the discounted cash flo(cid:90)method by the (cid:42)roup(cid:182)s Fair (cid:57)alue (cid:40)valuation System(cid:17)-  (cid:50)ther financial assets and liabilities(cid:29) (cid:55)he fair value of other financial assets and liabilities is computed using 
the discounted cash flo(cid:90) method(cid:17) (cid:43)o(cid:90)ever, in cases cash flo(cid:90) cannot be estimated reasonably, the (cid:42)roup

(cid:24)3(cid:17)    (cid:53)is(cid:78) (cid:48)anagement, (cid:38)ontinued(viii) Internal capital adequacy assessment process 
Internal capital adequacy assessment process is defined as the process that the (cid:42)roup aggregates significant risks, 
calculates its internal capital, compares the internal capital (cid:90)ith the available capital and assesses its internal capital 
adequacy(cid:17) (cid:55)he internal capital adequacy report including the assessment results at the end of the year is prepared 
and reported to the Risk (cid:48)anagement Policy Committee(cid:17) 
 
(cid:16) Internal capital adequacy assessment 
For the internal capital adequacy assessment, the (cid:42)roup calculates its aggregated internal capital by evaluating 
all significant risks and available capital considering the quality and components of capital, and then assesses the 
internal capital adequacy by comparing the aggregated internal capital (cid:90)ith the available capital(cid:17) 
 
In addition, the (cid:42)roup conducts periodic stress tests more than once every si(cid:91) months to assess potential (cid:90)eakness 
in  crisis  situations  and  uses  its  results  to  assess  the  internal  capital  adequacy(cid:17)  (cid:55)he  (cid:42)roup  assumes  the 
macroeconomic situation as three stages of (cid:181)normal(cid:16)pessimistic(cid:16)serious(cid:182) and is preparing countermeasures such 
as checking the adequacy of capital by each stage(cid:17) 
 
(cid:16) (cid:42)oal setting of internal capital management 
(cid:55)he (cid:42)roup sets up and manages an internal capital limit on an annual basis, through the approval of the Risk 
(cid:48)anagement Committee, to maintain internal capital adequacy by managing internal capital (integrated risks) 
(cid:90)ithin the e(cid:91)tent of available capital(cid:17) 
 
(cid:55)he prior year(cid:182)s internal capital, analysis of domestic and foreign environment changes in the current year, and 
the direction and si(cid:93)e of operations are all reflected in the goal setting of internal capital management to calculate 
the integrated internal capital scale(cid:17) (cid:48)oreover, Bank for International Settlements(BIS) capital adequacy ratio 
and risk appetite are taken into consideration in the goal setting of internal capital management(cid:17) 
 
(cid:16) Allocation of internal capital 
(cid:55)he (cid:42)roup(cid:182)s Risk (cid:48)anagement Committee approves entire internal capital and the Risk (cid:48)anagement Policy 
Committee allocates the capital to each segment and department, considering the e(cid:91)tent of possible risk faced 
and  si(cid:93)e  of  operations(cid:17)  (cid:55)he  allocated  internal  capital  is  monitored  regularly  and  managed  using  various 
management methods(cid:17) (cid:55)he results of monitoring and managing the allocated internal capital are reported to the 
Risk (cid:48)anagement Committee(cid:17) In case of any material changes in the (cid:42)roup(cid:182)s business plan or risk operation 
strategy, the (cid:42)roup adjusts the allocations elastically(cid:17)(cid:16) Composition of internal capital 
Internal  capital  comprises  all  the  significant  risks  of  the  (cid:42)roup  and  is  composed  of  quantifiable  and  non(cid:16)
quantifiable risks(cid:17) (cid:52)uantifiable risks are composed of credit risk, market risk, interest rate risk, operational risk 
and credit concentration risk, foreign currency settlement risk, and are risks measured quantitatively by applying 
reasonable methodology using objective data(cid:17) (cid:49)on(cid:16)quantifiable risks are composed of strategy risk, reputation 
risk, residual risk on asset securiti(cid:93)ation and furthermore(cid:17) (cid:49)on(cid:16)quantifiable risks are those risks that cannot be 
measured quantitatively because of lack of data or the absence of appropriate measuring methodologies(cid:17)(cid:11)2(cid:12) (cid:38)redit (cid:53)is(cid:78)(i) ConceptCredit  risk  can  be  defined  as  potential  loss  resulting  from  the  refusal  to  perform  obligations  or  default  of 
counterparties(cid:17) (cid:48)ore generally, it is used to refer to the possibility of loss from engaged bonds that cannot be

Summary of credit risk management 
(cid:55)he (cid:42)roup regards credit risk as the most significant risk area in its business operations, and accordingly, closely 
monitors its credit risk e(cid:91)posure(cid:17) (cid:55)he (cid:42)roup manages both credit risks at portfolio level and at individual credit 
level(cid:17) At portfolio level, the (cid:42)roup reduces credit concentration and restructures the portfolio in such a (cid:90)ay to 
ma(cid:91)imi(cid:93)e profitability considering the risk level(cid:17) (cid:55)o avoid credit concentration on a particular sector, the (cid:42)roup 
manages credit limits by client, group, and industry(cid:17) (cid:55)he (cid:42)roup also resets e(cid:91)posure management directives for 
each industry by conducting an industry credit evaluation t(cid:90)ice a year(cid:17)At  the  individual  credit  level,  the  relationship  manager  (R(cid:48)),  the  credit  officer  (C(cid:50))  and  the  Credit  Revie(cid:90) 
Committee manage each borro(cid:90)er(cid:182)s credit risk(cid:17)Post management and insolvent borrower management 
(cid:55)he (cid:42)roup monitors the borro(cid:90)er(cid:182)s credit rating from the date of the loan to the date of the final collection of debt 
consistently, and inspects the borro(cid:90)er(cid:182)s status regularly and frequently in order to prevent the generation of ne(cid:90) 
bad debts and to stabili(cid:93)e the number of debt recoveries(cid:17)  
 
In addition, an early (cid:90)arning system is operated to spot borro(cid:90)ers that are highly likely to be insolvent(cid:17) (cid:55)he early 
(cid:90)arning system provides financial information, financial transaction information, public information and market 
information of the borro(cid:90)er, and such information is used by the R(cid:48) and the C(cid:50) to monitor and manage changes 
in the borro(cid:90)er(cid:182)s credit rating(cid:17)  
 
(cid:56)nder the early (cid:90)arning system, a borro(cid:90)er that is highly likely to be insolvent is classified as an early (cid:90)arning 
borro(cid:90)er or a precautionary borro(cid:90)er (cid:55)he (cid:42)roup sets up a specific and applicable stabili(cid:93)ation plan for such a 
borro(cid:90)er  considering  the  borro(cid:90)er(cid:182)s  characteristics(cid:17)  Furthermore,  sub(cid:16)standard  borro(cid:90)ers  are  classified  as 
insolvent  borro(cid:90)ers,  and  are  managed  intensively  by  the  (cid:42)roup,  (cid:90)hich  takes  legal  proceedings,  disposals  or 
corporate turnaround measures if necessary(cid:17)  
 
Classification of asset soundness and provision of allowance for loss 
Classification of asset soundness is fulfilled by the analysis and assessment of credit risk(cid:17) (cid:55)he classification is used 
in order to provision an appropriate allo(cid:90)ance, prevent further occurrences of insolvent assets and promote the 
normali(cid:93)ation of e(cid:91)isting insolvent assets to enhance the stabili(cid:93)ation of asset operations(cid:17)  
 
Based on the Financial Supervisory Regulations of the Republic of Korea, the (cid:42)roup has established standards and 
guidelines on the classification of asset soundness, according to the For(cid:90)ard(cid:16)(cid:47)ooking Criteria (F(cid:47)C), (cid:90)hich reflects 
not only the borro(cid:90)er(cid:182)s past records of repayment but also their future debt repayment capability(cid:17)  
 
In conformity (cid:90)ith these standards, the (cid:42)roup classifies the soundness of its assets as (cid:179)normal(cid:180), (cid:179)precautionary(cid:180), 
(cid:179)substandard(cid:180), (cid:179)doubtful(cid:180), or (cid:179)estimated loss(cid:180) and differentiates the coverage ratio by the level of classification(cid:17)

conformity  (cid:90)ith  the  Banking  Act,  (cid:90)hich  is  based  on  the  implementation  of  Basel  III  on  December  2013,  the

Tier 1 capital (Common Equity Tier 1 + Additional Tier 1 capital) 
-  Common (cid:40)quity (cid:55)ier 1 
Regulatory  capital  that  represents  the  most  subordinated  claim  in  liquidation  of  the  (cid:42)roup,  takes  the  first  and 
proportionately greatest share of any losses as they occur, and (cid:90)hich principal is never repaid outside of liquidation 
meets the criteria for classification as common equity, including capital stock, capital surplus, retained earnings, 
and accumulated other comprehensive income as common equity (cid:55)ier 1(cid:17)  
 
-  Additional (cid:55)ier 1 capital 
Capital stock and capital surplus related to issuance of capital securities that are subordinated, have non(cid:16)cumulative 
and conditional dividends or interests, and have no maturity or step(cid:16)up conditions(cid:17)Tier 2 capital (Supplementary Tier 2 capital) 
Regulatory capital that fulfils supplementary capital adequacy requirements, and includes subordinated debt (cid:90)ith 
maturities over 5 years and allo(cid:90)ance for loan losses in conformity (cid:90)ith e(cid:91)ternal regulatory standards and internal

(i) Concept 
(cid:48)arket risk is defined as the possibility of potential loss on a trading position resulting from fluctuations in interest 
rates, foreign e(cid:91)change rates and the price of stocks and derivatives(cid:17) (cid:55)rading position is e(cid:91)posed to risks, such as 
interest rate, stock price, and foreign e(cid:91)change rate, etc(cid:17) (cid:49)on(cid:16)trading position is mostly e(cid:91)posed to interest rates(cid:17) 
Accordingly, the (cid:42)roup classifies market risks into those e(cid:91)posed from trading position or those e(cid:91)posed from non(cid:16)
trading position(cid:17)  
 
(ii) Market risks of trading positions
 
Management method on market risks arising from trading positions 
In estimating market risk, the Standardi(cid:93)ed Approach and the internal model are used(cid:17) (cid:55)he Standardi(cid:93)ed Approach 
is used to calculate the required capital from market risk and the internal model is used to manage risks internally(cid:17) 
Since (cid:45)uly 2007, the (cid:42)roup has measured one(cid:16)day (cid:57)aR through the historical simulation method using the time 
series data of past 250 days under a 99(cid:8) confidence level(cid:17) (cid:55)he calculated (cid:57)aR is monitored daily(cid:17) 
 
(cid:55)he (cid:42)roup sets total limit of market risk based on annual business plan, risk appetite and others and monitors (cid:57)aR 
limit of each department on a daily basis(cid:17)

Management method on market risks arising from non-trading positions 
(cid:55)he most critical market risk that arises in non(cid:16)trading position is the interest rate risk(cid:17) Interest rate risk is defined 
as the likely loss resulting from the unfavorable fluctuation of interest rate in the (cid:42)roup(cid:182)s financial condition and is 
measured by interest rate (cid:57)aR and interest rate (cid:40)aR(cid:17)  
 
Interest rate (cid:57)aR is the ma(cid:91)imum amount of decrease in net asset value resulting from the unfavorable fluctuation 
of interest rate(cid:17) Interest rate (cid:40)aR is the ma(cid:91)imum amount of decrease in net interest income resulting from the 
unfavorable fluctuation of interest rate for a year(cid:17)  
 
(cid:55)he (cid:42)roup(cid:182)s interest rate (cid:57)aR and interest rate (cid:40)aR are measured through the simulation of conclusive interest rate

(i) Concept 
(cid:47)iquidity risk is defined as the possibility of potential loss due to a temporary shortage in funds caused by a maturity 
mismatch or an une(cid:91)pected capital outlay(cid:17) (cid:47)iquidity risk soars (cid:90)hen funding rates rise, assets are sold belo(cid:90) a 
normal price, or a good investment opportunity is missed(cid:17)  
 
(ii) Approach to liquidity risk management

 (cid:48)id(cid:16) to long(cid:16)term foreign currency fund management ratio(cid:29) Foreign currency funding being repaid after 1 year (cid:18) 
Foreign currency lending being collected after 1 year (cid:59) 100Early warning indicator
